For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_Church_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Presbyterian Church (disambiguation)">Presbyterian Church (disambiguation)</a>.</div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the branch of Reformed Protestantism. For the method of church organization, see <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_polity" title="Presbyterian polity">Presbyterian polity</a>. For the broader Reformed tradition, see <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinism</a>.</div>\n<p class="mw-empty-elt">\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Burning-Bush-1.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Burning-Bush-1.gif/300px-Burning-Bush-1.gif" width="300" height="233" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Burning-Bush-1.gif/450px-Burning-Bush-1.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Burning-Bush-1.gif/600px-Burning-Bush-1.gif 2x" data-file-width="741" data-file-height="576" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Burning-Bush-1.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Burning_bush" title="Burning bush">burning bush</a> is a common symbol used by Presbyterian churches; here as used by the <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_Church_in_Ireland" title="Presbyterian Church in Ireland">Presbyterian Church in Ireland</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> inscription underneath translates as "burning but flourishing". In Presbyterianism, alternative versions of the motto are also used such as "burning, yet not consumed".</div></div></div>\n<table class="vertical-navbox plainlist" title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Presbyterianism</b> is a part of the <a href="/wiki/Reformed_tradition" class="mw-redirect" title="Reformed tradition">reformed tradition</a> within <a href="/wiki/Protestantism" title="Protestantism">Protestantism</a> which traces its origins to <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Britain</a>, particularly <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>.\n</p><p>Presbyterian churches derive their name from the <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_polity" title="Presbyterian polity">presbyterian</a> form of <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical_polity" title="Ecclesiastical polity">church government</a>, which is governed by representative assemblies of <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_elder" class="mw-redirect" title="Presbyterian elder">elders</a>. A great number of Reformed churches are organized this way, but the word <i>Presbyterian</i>, when capitalized, is often applied uniquely to churches that trace their roots to the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Scotland" title="Church of Scotland">Church of Scotland</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> as well as several <a href="/wiki/English_dissenters" class="mw-redirect" title="English dissenters">English dissenter</a> groups that formed during the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> Presbyterian theology typically emphasizes the sovereignty of God, the <a href="/wiki/Sola_scriptura" title="Sola scriptura">authority of the Scriptures</a>, and the necessity of <a href="/wiki/Grace_(Christianity)" class="mw-redirect" title="Grace (Christianity)">grace</a> through faith in Christ. Presbyterian church government was ensured in Scotland by the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1707" title="Acts of Union 1707">Acts of Union</a> in 1707<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> which created the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Great_Britain" title="Kingdom of Great Britain">Kingdom of Great Britain</a>. In fact, most Presbyterians found in England can trace a <a href="/wiki/Scottish_people" title="Scottish people">Scottish</a> connection, and the Presbyterian denomination was also taken to North America mostly by <a href="/wiki/Scotch-Irish_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Scotch-Irish American">Scots and Scots-Irish</a> immigrants. The Presbyterian denominations in Scotland hold to the <a href="/wiki/Reformed_theology" class="mw-redirect" title="Reformed theology">Reformed theology</a> of <a href="/wiki/John_Calvin" title="John Calvin">John Calvin</a> and his immediate successors, although there is a range of theological views within contemporary Presbyterianism. Local congregations of churches which use <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_polity" title="Presbyterian polity">presbyterian polity</a> are governed by <a href="/wiki/Session_(Presbyterianism)" title="Session (Presbyterianism)">sessions</a> made up of representatives of the congregation (<a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_elder" class="mw-redirect" title="Presbyterian elder">elders</a>); a <a href="/wiki/Conciliarity" title="Conciliarity">conciliar</a> approach which is found at other levels of decision-making (<a href="/wiki/Presbytery_(church_polity)" class="mw-redirect" title="Presbytery (church polity)">presbytery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_synod" class="mw-redirect" title="Presbyterian synod">synod</a> and <a href="/wiki/General_Assembly_(presbyterian_church)" class="mw-redirect" title="General Assembly (presbyterian church)">general assembly</a>).\n</p><p>The roots of Presbyterianism lie in the <a href="/wiki/Reformation" title="Reformation">Reformation</a> of the 16th century; the example of John Calvin\'s <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Geneva" class="mw-redirect" title="Republic of Geneva">Republic of Geneva</a> being particularly influential. Most Reformed churches that trace their history back to Scotland are either presbyterian or <a href="/wiki/Congregational_polity" class="mw-redirect" title="Congregational polity">congregationalist</a> in government. In the twentieth century, some Presbyterians played an important role in the <a href="/wiki/Ecumenism" title="Ecumenism">ecumenical movement</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/World_Council_of_Churches" title="World Council of Churches">World Council of Churches</a>. Many Presbyterian denominations have found ways of working together with other Reformed denominations and Christians of other traditions, especially in the <a href="/wiki/World_Communion_of_Reformed_Churches" title="World Communion of Reformed Churches">World Communion of Reformed Churches</a>. Some Presbyterian churches have entered into <a href="/wiki/United_and_uniting_churches" title="United and uniting churches">unions</a> with other churches, such as <a href="/wiki/Congregational_church" title="Congregational church">Congregationalists</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lutherans" class="mw-redirect" title="Lutherans">Lutherans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anglicans" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglicans">Anglicans</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Methodists" class="mw-redirect" title="Methodists">Methodists</a>. Presbyterians in the United States came largely from Scottish immigrants, <a href="/wiki/Scotch-Irish_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Scotch-Irish American">Scotch-Irish immigrants</a>, and also from New England <a href="/wiki/Yankee" title="Yankee">Yankee</a> communities that had originally been Congregational but changed because of an agreed-upon <a href="/wiki/Plan_of_Union_of_1801" title="Plan of Union of 1801">Plan of Union of 1801</a> for frontier areas.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> Along with <a href="/wiki/Episcopal_Church_(USA)" class="mw-redirect" title="Episcopal Church (USA)">Episcopalians</a>, Presbyterians tend to be considerably wealthier<sup id="cite_ref-THE_EPISCOPALIANS_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-THE_EPISCOPALIANS-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> and better educated (having more <a href="/wiki/Academic_degree" title="Academic degree">graduate</a> and post-graduate degrees per capita) than most other religious groups in United States,<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> and are disproportionately represented in the upper reaches of American business,<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> law and politics.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-47"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Presbyterian_identity">Presbyterian identity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Presbyterianism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Presbyterian identity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Iona_Abbey,_Entrance_and_St_John%27s_Cross._-_geograph.org.uk_-_113441.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Iona_Abbey%2C_Entrance_and_St_John%27s_Cross._-_geograph.org.uk_-_113441.jpg/200px-Iona_Abbey%2C_Entrance_and_St_John%27s_Cross._-_geograph.org.uk_-_113441.jpg" width="200" height="301" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Iona_Abbey%2C_Entrance_and_St_John%27s_Cross._-_geograph.org.uk_-_113441.jpg/300px-Iona_Abbey%2C_Entrance_and_St_John%27s_Cross._-_geograph.org.uk_-_113441.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Iona_Abbey%2C_Entrance_and_St_John%27s_Cross._-_geograph.org.uk_-_113441.jpg/400px-Iona_Abbey%2C_Entrance_and_St_John%27s_Cross._-_geograph.org.uk_-_113441.jpg 2x" data-file-width="425" data-file-height="640" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Iona_Abbey,_Entrance_and_St_John%27s_Cross._-_geograph.org.uk_-_113441.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Iona_Abbey" title="Iona Abbey">Iona Abbey</a> in Scotland was founded by <a href="/wiki/Saint_Columba" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Columba">Saint Columba</a>.</div></div></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Christianity_in_Scotland" title="History of Christianity in Scotland">History of Christianity in Scotland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Medieval_Scotland" title="Christianity in Medieval Scotland">Christianity in Medieval Scotland</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history">Early history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Presbyterianism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Presbyterian tradition, particularly that of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Scotland" title="Church of Scotland">Church of Scotland</a>, traces its early roots to the Church founded by <a href="/wiki/Saint_Columba" class="mw-redirect" title="Saint Columba">Saint Columba</a>, through the 6th century <a href="/wiki/Hiberno-Scottish_mission" title="Hiberno-Scottish mission">Hiberno-Scottish mission</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Atkins2016_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Atkins2016-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-TaylorAnderson1852_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TaylorAnderson1852-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Bradley2013_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bradley2013-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> Tracing their apostolic origin to <a href="/wiki/John_the_Apostle" title="John the Apostle">Saint John</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-DickensLewis1920_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DickensLewis1920-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Thomson1896_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomson1896-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Culdees" title="Culdees">Culdees</a> practiced <a href="/wiki/Christian_monasticism" title="Christian monasticism">Christian monasticism</a>, a key feature of <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Christianity" title="Celtic Christianity">Celtic Christianity</a> in the region, with a <a href="/wiki/Presbyter" title="Presbyter">presbyter</a> exercising "authority within the institution, while the different monastic institutions were independent of one another."<sup id="cite_ref-Mackay1902_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mackay1902-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Atkins2016_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Atkins2016-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HannrachainArmstrong2014_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HannrachainArmstrong2014-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> The Church in Scotland kept the Christian feast of Easter at a date different from the <a href="/wiki/See_of_Rome" class="mw-redirect" title="See of Rome">See of Rome</a> and its monks used a unique style of <a href="/wiki/Tonsure" title="Tonsure">tonsure</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Rankin1884_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rankin1884-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Synod_of_Whitby" title="Synod of Whitby">Synod of Whitby</a> in 664, however, ended these distinctives as it ruled "that Easter would be celebrated according to the Roman date, not the Celtic date."<sup id="cite_ref-Skinner1999_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Skinner1999-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Although Roman influence came to dominate the Church in Scotland,<sup id="cite_ref-Skinner1999_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Skinner1999-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> certain Celtic influences remained in the Scottish Church,<sup id="cite_ref-Eggins2015_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Eggins2015-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> such as "the singing of metrical psalms, many of them set to old Celtic Christianity Scottish traditional and folk tunes", which later became a "distinctive part of Scottish Presbyterian worship".<sup id="cite_ref-Bowden2005_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bowden2005-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Presbyterianism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Knox_woodcut.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/John_Knox_woodcut.jpg/220px-John_Knox_woodcut.jpg" width="220" height="260" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/John_Knox_woodcut.jpg/330px-John_Knox_woodcut.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/John_Knox_woodcut.jpg/440px-John_Knox_woodcut.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1859" data-file-height="2199" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:John_Knox_woodcut.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/John_Knox" title="John Knox">John Knox</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Presbyterian history is part of the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Christianity" title="History of Christianity">history of Christianity</a>, but the beginning of Presbyterianism as a distinct movement occurred during the 16th-century <a href="/wiki/Protestant_Reformation" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestant Reformation">Protestant Reformation</a>. As the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> resisted the reformers, several different theological movements splintered from the Church and bore different denominations. Presbyterianism was especially influenced by the French theologian <a href="/wiki/John_Calvin" title="John Calvin">John Calvin</a>, who is credited with the development of <a href="/wiki/Reformed_theology" class="mw-redirect" title="Reformed theology">Reformed theology</a>, and the work of <a href="/wiki/John_Knox" title="John Knox">John Knox</a>, a Scotsman and a Roman Catholic Priest, who studied with Calvin in Geneva, Switzerland. He brought back Reformed teachings to Scotland. The Presbyterian church traces its ancestry back primarily to England and Scotland. In August 1560 the <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_Scotland" title="Parliament of Scotland">Parliament of Scotland</a> adopted the <i><a href="/wiki/Scots_Confession" title="Scots Confession">Scots Confession</a></i> as the creed of the Scottish Kingdom. In December 1560, the <i><a href="/wiki/First_Book_of_Discipline" class="mw-redirect" title="First Book of Discipline">First Book of Discipline</a></i> was published, outlining important doctrinal issues but also establishing regulations for church government, including the creation of ten ecclesiastical districts with appointed superintendents which later became known as <a href="/wiki/Presbytery_(church_polity)" class="mw-redirect" title="Presbytery (church polity)">presbyteries</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In time, the Scots Confession would be supplanted by the <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Confession_of_Faith" title="Westminster Confession of Faith">Westminster Confession of Faith</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Larger_Catechism" title="Westminster Larger Catechism">Larger</a> and <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Shorter_Catechism" title="Westminster Shorter Catechism">Shorter Catechisms</a>, which were formulated by the <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Assembly" title="Westminster Assembly">Westminster Assembly</a> between 1643 and 1649.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Presbyterianism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>Presbyterians distinguish themselves from other denominations by <a href="/wiki/Doctrine" title="Doctrine">doctrine</a>, institutional organization (or "church order") and <a href="/wiki/Worship" title="Worship">worship</a>; often using a "Book of Order" to regulate common practice and order. The origins of the Presbyterian churches are in <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinism</a>. Many branches of Presbyterianism are remnants of previous splits from larger groups. Some of the splits have been due to doctrinal controversy, while some have been caused by disagreement concerning the degree to which those ordained to church office should be required to agree with the <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Confession_of_Faith" title="Westminster Confession of Faith">Westminster Confession of Faith</a>, which historically serves as an important confessional document \xe2\x80\x93 second only to the Bible, yet directing particularities in the standardization and translation of the Bible \xe2\x80\x93 in Presbyterian churches.\n</p><p>Presbyterians place great importance upon education and lifelong learning. Continuous study of the scriptures, theological writings, and understanding and interpretation of church doctrine are embodied in several statements of faith and catechisms formally adopted by various branches of the church, often referred to as "<a href="/wiki/Subordinate_standard" title="Subordinate standard">subordinate standards</a>". It is generally considered that the point of such learning is to enable one to put one\'s faith into practice; many Presbyterians generally exhibit their faith in action as well as words, by generosity, hospitality, as well as proclaiming the gospel of Christ.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Government">Government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Presbyterianism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_church_governance" class="mw-redirect" title="Presbyterian church governance">Presbyterian church governance</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lorimer,_Ordination.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Lorimer%2C_Ordination.jpg/220px-Lorimer%2C_Ordination.jpg" width="220" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Lorimer%2C_Ordination.jpg/330px-Lorimer%2C_Ordination.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Lorimer%2C_Ordination.jpg/440px-Lorimer%2C_Ordination.jpg 2x" data-file-width="646" data-file-height="500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lorimer,_Ordination.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Ordination of Elders in a Scottish Kirk, by <a href="/wiki/John_Henry_Lorimer" title="John Henry Lorimer">John Henry Lorimer</a>, 1891. <a href="/wiki/National_Gallery_of_Scotland" class="mw-redirect" title="National Gallery of Scotland">National Gallery of Scotland</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Presbyterian government is by councils (known as <i>courts</i>) of elders. Teaching and ruling elders are ordained and convene in the lowest council known as a <a href="/wiki/Session_(Presbyterianism)" title="Session (Presbyterianism)"><i>session</i></a> or <i><a href="/wiki/Consistory_(Protestantism)#Reformed_usage" title="Consistory (Protestantism)">consistory</a></i> responsible for the discipline, nurture, and mission of the local <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/congregation" class="extiw" title="wiktionary:congregation">congregation</a>. Teaching elders (pastors) have responsibility for teaching, worship, and performing sacraments. Pastors are called by individual congregations. A congregation issues a call for the pastor\'s service, but this call must be ratified by the local presbytery.  The Pastor is a Teaching Elder, and voting member and Moderator of the Session, but is not a member of the church.\n</p><p>Ruling elders are laymen and laywomen who are elected by the congregation and ordained to serve with the teaching elders, assuming responsibility for nurture and leadership of the congregation. Often, especially in larger congregations, the elders delegate the practicalities of buildings, finance, and temporal ministry to the needy in the congregation to a distinct group of officers (sometimes called deacons, which are ordained in some denominations). This group may variously be known as a "Deacon Board", "Board of Deacons" "Diaconate", or "Deacons\' Court".  These are sometimes known as "presbyters" to the full congregation.\n</p><p>Above the sessions exist presbyteries, which have area responsibilities. These are composed of teaching elders and ruling elders from each of the constituent congregations. The presbytery sends representatives to a broader regional or national assembly, generally known as the <a href="/wiki/General_Assembly_(presbyterian_church)" class="mw-redirect" title="General Assembly (presbyterian church)">General Assembly</a>, although an intermediate level of a <i><a href="/wiki/Synod" title="Synod">synod</a></i> sometimes exists. This congregation / <a href="/wiki/Presbytery_(presbyterian_church)" class="mw-redirect" title="Presbytery (presbyterian church)">presbytery</a> / <a href="/wiki/Synod" title="Synod">synod</a> / <a href="/wiki/General_Assembly_(presbyterian_church)" class="mw-redirect" title="General Assembly (presbyterian church)">general assembly</a> schema is based on the historical structure of the larger Presbyterian churches, such as the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Scotland" title="Church of Scotland">Church of Scotland</a> or the <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_Church_(U.S.A.)" class="mw-redirect" title="Presbyterian Church (U.S.A.)">Presbyterian Church (U.S.A.)</a>; some bodies, such as the <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_Church_in_America" title="Presbyterian Church in America">Presbyterian Church in America</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_Church_in_Ireland" title="Presbyterian Church in Ireland">Presbyterian Church in Ireland</a>, skip one of the steps between congregation and General Assembly, and usually the step skipped is the Synod. The <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Scotland" title="Church of Scotland">Church of Scotland</a> abolished the Synod in 1993.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Presbyterian governance is practised by Presbyterian denominations and also by many other <a href="/wiki/Reformed_churches" class="mw-redirect" title="Reformed churches">Reformed churches</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Doctrine">Doctrine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Presbyterianism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Doctrine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Original_research" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/40px-Ambox_important.svg.png" width="40" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/60px-Ambox_important.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/80px-Ambox_important.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="40" data-file-height="40" /></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section  <b>possibly contains <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research" title="Wikipedia:No original research">original research</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Presbyterianism&amp;action=edit">improve it</a> by <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verifying</a> the claims made and adding <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#Inline_citations" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">inline citations</a>. Statements consisting only of original research should be removed.</span>  <small><i>(September 2014)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Reformed_theology" class="mw-redirect" title="Reformed theology">Reformed theology</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Celtic_cross_draped_for_Easter.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Celtic_cross_draped_for_Easter.jpg/220px-Celtic_cross_draped_for_Easter.jpg" width="220" height="331" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Celtic_cross_draped_for_Easter.jpg/330px-Celtic_cross_draped_for_Easter.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/36/Celtic_cross_draped_for_Easter.jpg 2x" data-file-width="399" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Celtic_cross_draped_for_Easter.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Celtic cross draped for Easter at a Presbyterian church</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Snow_covered_Celtic_cross_in_memorial_garden.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Snow_covered_Celtic_cross_in_memorial_garden.jpg/220px-Snow_covered_Celtic_cross_in_memorial_garden.jpg" width="220" height="331" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Snow_covered_Celtic_cross_in_memorial_garden.jpg/330px-Snow_covered_Celtic_cross_in_memorial_garden.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/88/Snow_covered_Celtic_cross_in_memorial_garden.jpg 2x" data-file-width="399" data-file-height="600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Snow_covered_Celtic_cross_in_memorial_garden.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Snow-covered Celtic cross in a Presbyterian memorial garden</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:USVA_headstone_emb-04.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/USVA_headstone_emb-04.svg/170px-USVA_headstone_emb-04.svg.png" width="170" height="241" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/USVA_headstone_emb-04.svg/255px-USVA_headstone_emb-04.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/USVA_headstone_emb-04.svg/340px-USVA_headstone_emb-04.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="412" data-file-height="584" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:USVA_headstone_emb-04.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"Presbyterian Cross", used by the National Cemetery Administration of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Veterans_Affairs" title="United States Department of Veterans Affairs">United States Department of Veterans Affairs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Presbyterianism is historically a confessional tradition. This has two implications. The obvious one is that confessional churches express their faith in the form of "confessions of faith," which have some level of authoritative status. However this is based on a more subtle point: In confessional churches, theology is not solely an individual matter. While individuals are encouraged to understand Scripture, and may challenge the current institutional understanding, theology is carried out by the community as a whole. It is this community understanding of theology that is expressed in confessions.<sup id="cite_ref-lostsoul_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lostsoul-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>However, there has arisen a spectrum of approaches to <a href="/wiki/Confessionalism_(religion)" title="Confessionalism (religion)">confessionalism</a>.  The manner of subscription, or the degree to which the official standards establish the actual doctrine of the church, turns out to be a practical matter.  That is, the decisions rendered in ordination and in the courts of the church largely determine what the church means, representing the whole, by its adherence to the doctrinal standard.\n</p><p>Some Presbyterian traditions adopt only the <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Confession_of_Faith" title="Westminster Confession of Faith">Westminster Confession of Faith</a> as the doctrinal standard to which teaching elders are required to subscribe, in contrast to the <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Larger_Catechism" title="Westminster Larger Catechism">Larger</a> and <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Shorter_Catechism" title="Westminster Shorter Catechism">Shorter</a> catechisms, which are approved for use in instruction.  Many Presbyterian denominations, especially in North America, have adopted all of the <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Standards" title="Westminster Standards">Westminster Standards</a> as their standard of doctrine which is subordinate to the Bible.  These documents are <a href="/wiki/Calvinist" class="mw-redirect" title="Calvinist">Calvinistic</a> in their doctrinal orientation. The <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_Church_in_Canada" title="Presbyterian Church in Canada">Presbyterian Church in Canada</a> retains the Westminster Confession of Faith in its original form, while admitting the historical period in which it was written should be understood when it is read.\n</p><p>The Westminster Confession is "The principal <a href="/wiki/Subordinate_standard" title="Subordinate standard">subordinate standard</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Scotland" title="Church of Scotland">Church of Scotland</a>" but "with due regard to liberty of opinion in points which do not enter into the substance of the Faith" (V).  This formulation represents many years of struggle over the extent to which the confession reflects the Word of God and the struggle of conscience of those who came to believe it did not fully do so (e.g. <a href="/wiki/William_Robertson_Smith" title="William Robertson Smith">William Robertson Smith</a>).  Some Presbyterian Churches, such as the <a href="/wiki/Free_Church_of_Scotland_(post_1900)" class="mw-redirect" title="Free Church of Scotland (post 1900)">Free Church of Scotland</a>, have no such "<a href="/wiki/Conscience" title="Conscience">conscience</a> clause".\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_Church_(U.S.A.)" class="mw-redirect" title="Presbyterian Church (U.S.A.)">Presbyterian Church (U.S.A.)</a> has adopted the <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Confessions" title="Book of Confessions">Book of Confessions</a></i>, which reflects the inclusion of other <a href="/wiki/Reformed_confessions" class="mw-redirect" title="Reformed confessions">Reformed confessions</a> in addition to the Westminster Standards.  These other documents include ancient creedal statements (the <a href="/wiki/Nicene_Creed" title="Nicene Creed">Nicene Creed</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Apostles%27_Creed" title="Apostles&#39; Creed">Apostles\' Creed</a>), 16th-century Reformed confessions (the <a href="/wiki/Scots_Confession" title="Scots Confession">Scots Confession</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Heidelberg_Catechism" title="Heidelberg Catechism">Heidelberg Catechism</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Second_Helvetic_Confession" class="mw-redirect" title="Second Helvetic Confession">Second Helvetic Confession</a>), and 20th century documents (<a href="/wiki/Barmen_Declaration" title="Barmen Declaration">The Theological Declaration of Barmen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Confession_of_1967" title="Confession of 1967">Confession of 1967</a> and <a href="/wiki/A_Brief_Statement_of_Faith" class="mw-redirect" title="A Brief Statement of Faith">A Brief Statement of Faith</a>).\n</p><p>The Presbyterian Church in Canada developed the confessional document <i>Living Faith</i> (1984) and retains it as a subordinate standard of the denomination. It is confessional in format, yet like the Westminster Confession, draws attention back to original Bible text.\n</p><p>Presbyterians in Ireland who rejected Calvinism and the Westminster Confessions formed the <a href="/wiki/Non-subscribing_Presbyterian_Church_of_Ireland" title="Non-subscribing Presbyterian Church of Ireland">Non-subscribing Presbyterian Church of Ireland</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Worship_and_sacraments">Worship and sacraments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Presbyterianism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Worship and sacraments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Worship">Worship</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Presbyterianism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Worship">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_worship" title="Presbyterian worship">Presbyterian worship</a></div>\n<p>Presbyterian denominations that trace their heritage to the British Isles usually organise their church services inspired by the principles in the <a href="/wiki/Directory_of_Public_Worship" class="mw-redirect" title="Directory of Public Worship">Directory of Public Worship</a>, developed by the <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Assembly" title="Westminster Assembly">Westminster Assembly</a> in the 1640s.  This directory documented <a href="/wiki/Reformed_worship" title="Reformed worship">Reformed worship</a> practices and theology adopted and developed over the preceding century by British <a href="/wiki/Puritan" class="mw-redirect" title="Puritan">Puritans</a>, initially guided by John Calvin and John Knox.  It was enacted as law by the Scottish Parliament, and became one of the foundational documents of Presbyterian church legislation elsewhere.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Presbyterian_Catechising.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Presbyterian_Catechising.jpg/220px-Presbyterian_Catechising.jpg" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Presbyterian_Catechising.jpg/330px-Presbyterian_Catechising.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Presbyterian_Catechising.jpg/440px-Presbyterian_Catechising.jpg 2x" data-file-width="944" data-file-height="613" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Presbyterian_Catechising.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Presbyterian <a href="/wiki/Catechesis" title="Catechesis">catechising</a>, 19th century</div></div></div>\n<p>Historically, the driving principle in the development of the standards of Presbyterian worship is the <a href="/wiki/Regulative_principle_of_worship" title="Regulative principle of worship">Regulative principle of worship</a>, which specifies that (in worship), what is not commanded is forbidden.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Over subsequent centuries, many Presbyterian churches modified these prescriptions by introducing hymnody, instrumental accompaniment, and ceremonial <a href="/wiki/Vestment" title="Vestment">vestments</a> into worship. However, there is not one fixed  "Presbyterian" worship style. Although there are set services for the <a href="/wiki/Lord%27s_Day" title="Lord&#39;s Day">Lord\'s Day</a> in keeping with <a href="/wiki/First-day_Sabbatarianism" class="mw-redirect" title="First-day Sabbatarianism">first-day Sabbatarianism</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Wigley1980_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wigley1980-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> one can find a service to be evangelical and even revivalist in tone (especially in some conservative denominations), or strongly liturgical, approximating the practices of <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheranism</a> or <a href="/wiki/Anglicanism" title="Anglicanism">Anglicanism</a> (especially where Scottish tradition is esteemed),<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (January 2010)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> or semi-formal, allowing for a balance of hymns, preaching, and congregational participation (favored by probably most American Presbyterians).  Most Presbyterian churches follow the traditional liturgical year and observe the traditional holidays, holy seasons, such as Advent, Christmas, Ash Wednesday, Holy Week, Easter, Pentecost, etc.  They also make use of the appropriate seasonal liturgical colors, etc.  Many incorporate ancient liturgical prayers and responses into the communion services and follow a daily, seasonal, and festival lectionary. Other Presbyterians, however, such as the <a href="/wiki/Reformed_Presbyterian_churches" title="Reformed Presbyterian churches">Reformed Presbyterians</a>, would practice <a href="/wiki/A_cappella" title="A cappella">a cappella</a> <a href="/wiki/Exclusive_psalmody" title="Exclusive psalmody">exclusive psalmody</a>, as well as eschew the celebration of holy days.\n</p><p>Among the <a href="/wiki/Paleo-orthodoxy" title="Paleo-orthodoxy">paleo-orthodox</a> and <a href="/wiki/Emerging_church" title="Emerging church">emerging church</a> movements in Protestant and evangelical churches, in which some Presbyterians are involved, clergy are moving away from the traditional black <a href="/wiki/Geneva_gown" title="Geneva gown">Geneva gown</a> to such vestments as the <a href="/wiki/Alb" title="Alb">alb</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chasuble" title="Chasuble">chasuble</a>, but also <a href="/wiki/Cassock" title="Cassock">cassock</a> and <a href="/wiki/Surplice" title="Surplice">surplice</a> (typically a full length Old English style surplice which resembles the <a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celtic</a> alb, an ungirdled liturgical tunic of the old <a href="/wiki/Gallican_Rite" title="Gallican Rite">Gallican Rite</a>), which some, particularly those identifying with the Liturgical Renewal Movement, hold to be more ancient and representative of a more ecumenical past.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sacraments">Sacraments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Presbyterianism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Sacraments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Reformed_baptismal_theology" title="Reformed baptismal theology">Reformed baptismal theology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lord%27s_Supper_in_Reformed_theology" title="Lord&#39;s Supper in Reformed theology">Lord\'s Supper in Reformed theology</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:A_Scottish_Sacrament.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/A_Scottish_Sacrament.jpg/220px-A_Scottish_Sacrament.jpg" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/A_Scottish_Sacrament.jpg/330px-A_Scottish_Sacrament.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/A_Scottish_Sacrament.jpg/440px-A_Scottish_Sacrament.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="781" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:A_Scottish_Sacrament.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>A Scottish Sacrament</i>, by Henry John Dobson</div></div></div>\n<p>Presbyterians traditionally have held the Worship position that there are only two <a href="/wiki/Sacraments" class="mw-redirect" title="Sacraments">sacraments</a>:\n</p>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Baptism" title="Baptism">Baptism</a>, in which they would <a href="/wiki/Infant_baptism" title="Infant baptism">baptize infants</a>, as well as unbaptized adults by the <a href="/wiki/Aspersion" title="Aspersion">Aspersion</a> (sprinkling) or <a href="/wiki/Affusion" title="Affusion">Affusion</a> (pouring) method, rather than the <a href="/wiki/Baptism#Immersion" title="Baptism">Immersion</a> method.</li>\n<li>The <a href="/wiki/Eucharist" title="Eucharist">Lord\'s Supper</a> (also known as Communion), in which they would believe that Christ is present in the bread and wine through the Holy Spirit, as opposed to being locally present.</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Presbyterianism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cold_Spring_Presby_from_SE.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Cold_Spring_Presby_from_SE.JPG/260px-Cold_Spring_Presby_from_SE.JPG" width="260" height="195" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Cold_Spring_Presby_from_SE.JPG/390px-Cold_Spring_Presby_from_SE.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Cold_Spring_Presby_from_SE.JPG/520px-Cold_Spring_Presby_from_SE.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4320" data-file-height="3240" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cold_Spring_Presby_from_SE.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Cold_Spring_Presbyterian_Church" title="Cold Spring Presbyterian Church">Cold Spring Presbyterian Church</a> near Cape May, New Jersey, rebuilt 1823</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:4th_Presbyterian_Chicago_2004-11_img_2602.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/4th_Presbyterian_Chicago_2004-11_img_2602.jpg/260px-4th_Presbyterian_Chicago_2004-11_img_2602.jpg" width="260" height="333" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/4th_Presbyterian_Chicago_2004-11_img_2602.jpg/390px-4th_Presbyterian_Chicago_2004-11_img_2602.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/4th_Presbyterian_Chicago_2004-11_img_2602.jpg/520px-4th_Presbyterian_Chicago_2004-11_img_2602.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1129" data-file-height="1448" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:4th_Presbyterian_Chicago_2004-11_img_2602.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Fourth_Presbyterian_Church_(Chicago)" title="Fourth Presbyterian Church (Chicago)">Fourth Presbyterian Church (Chicago)</a>, built 1914</div></div></div>\n<p>Early Presbyterians were careful to distinguish between the "church," which referred to the <i>members</i>, and the "meeting house," which was the building in which the church met.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Until the late 19th century, very few Presbyterians ever referred to their buildings as "churches." Presbyterians believed that meeting-houses (now called churches) are buildings to support the worship of God. The <a href="/wiki/Interior_decoration" class="mw-redirect" title="Interior decoration">decor</a> in some instances was austere so as not to detract from worship. Early Presbyterian meeting-houses were extremely plain. No stained glass, no elaborate furnishings, and no images were to be found in the meeting-house. The pulpit, often raised so as only to be accessible by a staircase, was the centerpiece of the building. But these were not the standard characteristics of the mainline Presbyterians. These were more of the wave of Presbyterians that were influenced by the Puritans and simplicity.\n</p><p>In the late 19th century a gradual shift began to occur. Prosperous congregations built imposing churches, such as <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Presbyterian_Church_of_Chicago" class="mw-redirect" title="Fourth Presbyterian Church of Chicago">Fourth Presbyterian Church of Chicago</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Avenue_Presbyterian_Church" title="Fifth Avenue Presbyterian Church">Fifth Avenue Presbyterian Church</a> in New York City, and many others.\n</p><p>Usually a Presbyterian church will not have statues of saints, nor the ornate altar more typical of a <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic</a> church. Instead, one will find a "communion table," usually on the same level as the congregation.  There may be a rail between the communion table and the "Chancel" behind it, which may contain a more decorative altar-type table, choir loft, or choir stalls, lectern and clergy area.  The altar is called the communion table and the altar area is called the Chancel by Presbyterians. In a Presbyterian (Reformed Church) there may be an altar cross, either on the communion table or on a table in the chancel. By using the "empty" cross, or cross of the resurrection, Presbyterians emphasize the resurrection and that Christ is not continually dying, but died once and is alive for all eternity. Some Presbyterian church buildings are often decorated with a cross that has a circle around the center, or Celtic cross.  This not only emphasized the resurrection, but also acknowledges historical aspects of Presbyterianism.  A baptismal font will be located either at the entrance or near the chancel area. Presbyterian architecture generally makes significant use of symbolism. You may also find decorative and ornate stained glass windows depicting scenes from the bible. Some Presbyterian churches will also have ornate statues of Christ or Graven Scenes from the Last Supper located behind the Chancel. St. Giles Cathedral ( Church of Scotland- The Mother Church of Presbyterians) does have a Crucifix next to one of the Pulpits that hangs alongside. The image of Christ is more of faint image and more modern design.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="By_region">By region</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Presbyterianism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: By region">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="United_Kingdom_and_Europe">United Kingdom and Europe</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Presbyterianism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: United Kingdom and Europe">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Scotland">Scotland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Presbyterianism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Scotland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p><a href="/wiki/John_Knox" title="John Knox">John Knox</a> (1505\xe2\x80\x931572), a <a href="/wiki/Scottish_people" title="Scottish people">Scot</a> who had spent time studying under <a href="/wiki/John_Calvin" title="John Calvin">Calvin</a> in Geneva, returned to <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a> and urged his countrymen to reform the Church in line with <a href="/wiki/Calvinism" title="Calvinism">Calvinist</a> doctrines. After a period of religious convulsion and political conflict culminating in a victory for the <a href="/wiki/Lords_of_the_Congregation" title="Lords of the Congregation">Protestant party</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Leith" title="Siege of Leith">Siege of Leith</a> the authority of the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> was abolished in favour of <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Reformation" title="Scottish Reformation">Reformation</a> by the legislation of the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Reformation_Parliament" title="Scottish Reformation Parliament">Scottish Reformation Parliament</a> in 1560. The Church was eventually organised by <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Melville" title="Andrew Melville">Andrew Melville</a> along Presbyterian lines to become the national <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Scotland" title="Church of Scotland">Church of Scotland</a>. <a href="/wiki/King_James_VI_and_I" class="mw-redirect" title="King James VI and I">King James VI and I</a> moved the Church of Scotland towards an episcopal form of government, and in 1637, James\' successor, <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_England" title="Charles I of England">Charles I</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Laud" title="William Laud">William Laud</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Archbishop of Canterbury</a>, attempted to force the Church of Scotland to use the <a href="/wiki/Book_of_Common_Prayer" title="Book of Common Prayer">Book of Common Prayer</a>. What resulted was an armed insurrection, with many Scots signing the <i><a href="/wiki/Solemn_League_and_Covenant" title="Solemn League and Covenant">Solemn League and Covenant</a></i>. The <a href="/wiki/Covenanter" title="Covenanter">Covenanters</a> would serve as the government of Scotland for nearly a decade, and would also send military support to the <a href="/wiki/Roundhead" title="Roundhead">Parliamentarians</a> during the <a href="/wiki/English_Civil_War" title="English Civil War">English Civil War</a>. Following the restoration of the monarchy in 1660, <a href="/wiki/Charles_II_of_England" title="Charles II of England">Charles II</a>, despite the initial support that he received from the Covenanters, reinstated an episcopal form of government on the church.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Covenanters_in_a_Glen.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Covenanters_in_a_Glen.jpg/220px-Covenanters_in_a_Glen.jpg" width="220" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Covenanters_in_a_Glen.jpg/330px-Covenanters_in_a_Glen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Covenanters_in_a_Glen.jpg/440px-Covenanters_in_a_Glen.jpg 2x" data-file-width="944" data-file-height="731" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Covenanters_in_a_Glen.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An illegal <a href="/wiki/Conventicle" title="Conventicle">conventicle</a>. <i>Covenanters in a Glen</i>.</div></div></div>\n<p>However, with the <a href="/wiki/Glorious_Revolution" title="Glorious Revolution">Glorious Revolution</a> of 1688 the Church of Scotland was finally unequivocally recognised as a Presbyterian institution by the monarch due to Scottish Presbyterian support for the aforementioned revolution and the <a href="/wiki/Acts_of_Union_1707" title="Acts of Union 1707">Acts of Union 1707</a> between Scotland and England guaranteed the Church of Scotland\'s form of government. However, legislation by the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom_parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="United Kingdom parliament">United Kingdom parliament</a> allowing <a href="/wiki/Patronage" title="Patronage">patronage</a> led to splits in the Church. In 1733, a group of ministers <a href="/wiki/First_Secession" title="First Secession">seceded from the Church of Scotland</a> to form the Associate Presbytery, another group seceded in 1761 to form the <a href="/wiki/Relief_Church" title="Relief Church">Relief Church</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Disruption_of_1843" title="Disruption of 1843">Disruption of 1843</a> led to the formation of the <a href="/wiki/Free_Church_of_Scotland_(1843-1900)" class="mw-redirect" title="Free Church of Scotland (1843-1900)">Free Church of Scotland</a>. Further splits took place, especially over theological issues, but most Presbyterians in Scotland were reunited by 1929 union of the established Church of Scotland and the <a href="/wiki/United_Free_Church_of_Scotland" title="United Free Church of Scotland">United Free Church of Scotland</a>.\n</p><p>The Presbyterian denominations in Scotland today are the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Scotland" title="Church of Scotland">Church of Scotland</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Free_Church_of_Scotland_(post_1900)" class="mw-redirect" title="Free Church of Scotland (post 1900)">Free Church of Scotland</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_Free_Church_of_Scotland" title="United Free Church of Scotland">United Free Church of Scotland</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Free_Church_of_Scotland_(Continuing)" title="Free Church of Scotland (Continuing)">Free Church of Scotland (Continuing)</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Free_Presbyterian_Church_of_Scotland" title="Free Presbyterian Church of Scotland">Free Presbyterian Church of Scotland</a>, the Associated Presbyterian Church (<a href="/wiki/Associated_Presbyterian_Churches" title="Associated Presbyterian Churches">Associated Presbyterian Churches</a>), and the <a href="/wiki/Reformed_Presbyterian_Church_of_Scotland" title="Reformed Presbyterian Church of Scotland">Reformed Presbyterian Church of Scotland</a>.\n</p><p>Within Scotland the term <a href="/wiki/Kirk" title="Kirk">kirk</a> is usually used to refer to a local Presbyterian church. Informally, the term \'The Kirk\' refers to the Church of Scotland. Some of the values and ideals espoused in Scottish presbyterian denominations can be reflected in this reference in a book from Norman Drummond, chaplain to the Queen in Scotland.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="England">England</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Presbyterianism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: England">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/English_Presbyterianism" title="English Presbyterianism">English Presbyterianism</a></div>\n<p>In England, Presbyterianism was established in secret in 1592. <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Cartwright_(Puritan)" class="mw-redirect" title="Thomas Cartwright (Puritan)">Thomas Cartwright</a> is thought to be the first Presbyterian in England. Cartwright\'s controversial lectures at <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Cambridge University">Cambridge University</a> condemning the <a href="/wiki/Episcopal_polity" title="Episcopal polity">episcopal</a> hierarchy of the Elizabethan Church led to his deprivation of his post by Archbishop <a href="/wiki/John_Whitgift" title="John Whitgift">John Whitgift</a> and his emigration abroad. Between 1645 and 1648, a series of ordinances of the <a href="/wiki/Long_Parliament" title="Long Parliament">Long Parliament</a> established Presbyterianism as the polity of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_England" title="Church of England">Church of England</a>.  Presbyterian government was established in London and Lancashire and in a few other places in England, although Presbyterian hostility to the execution of <a href="/wiki/Charles_I_of_England" title="Charles I of England">Charles I</a> and the establishment of the republican <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_England" title="Commonwealth of England">Commonwealth of England</a> meant that Parliament never enforced the Presbyterian system in England.  The re-establishment of the monarchy in 1660 brought the return of <a href="/wiki/Episcopal_polity" title="Episcopal polity">Episcopal</a> church government in England (and in Scotland for a short time); but the Presbyterian church in England continued in Non-Conformity, outside of the established church. In 1719 a major split, the <a href="/wiki/Salter%27s_Hall_controversy" class="mw-redirect" title="Salter&#39;s Hall controversy">Salter\'s Hall controversy</a>, occurred; with the majority siding with <a href="/wiki/Nontrinitarian" class="mw-redirect" title="Nontrinitarian">nontrinitarian</a> views. <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Bradbury" title="Thomas Bradbury">Thomas Bradbury</a> published several sermons bearing on the controversy, and in 1719, "An answer to the reproaches cast on the dissenting ministers who subscribed their belief of the Eternal Trinity.". By the 18th century many English Presbyterian congregations had become <a href="/wiki/Unitarianism" title="Unitarianism">Unitarian</a> in doctrine.\n</p><p>A number of new Presbyterian Churches were founded by <a href="/wiki/Scottish_people" title="Scottish people">Scottish</a> immigrants to England in the 19th century and later.  Following the \'Disruption\' in 1843 many of those linked to the Church of Scotland eventually joined what became the Presbyterian Church of England in 1876.  Some, that is Crown Court (Covent Garden, London), St Andrew\'s (Stepney, London) and <a href="/wiki/Swallow_Street" title="Swallow Street">Swallow Street</a> (London), did not join the English denomination, which is why there are Church of Scotland congregations in England such as those at <a href="/wiki/Crown_Court_Church" title="Crown Court Church">Crown Court</a>, and <a href="/wiki/St_Columba%27s_Church,_London" title="St Columba&#39;s Church, London">St Columba\'s</a>, Pont Street (Knightsbridge) in London. There is also a congregation in the heart of London\'s financial district called London City Presbyterian Church that is also affiliated with Free Church of Scotland.\n</p><p>In 1972, the Presbyterian Church of England (PCofE) united with the Congregational Church in England and Wales to form the <a href="/wiki/United_Reformed_Church" title="United Reformed Church">United Reformed Church</a> (URC).  Among the congregations the PCofE brought to the URC were Tunley (Lancashire), <a href="/wiki/Aston_Tirrold" title="Aston Tirrold">Aston Tirrold</a> (Oxfordshire) and John Knox Presbyterian Church, Stepney, London (now part of Stepney Meeting House URC) \xe2\x80\x93 these are among the sole survivors today of the English Presbyterian churches of the 17th century. The URC also has a presence in Scotland, mostly of former <a href="/wiki/Congregational_church" title="Congregational church">Congregationalist</a> Churches.  Two former Presbyterian congregations, St Columba\'s, Cambridge (founded in 1879), and St Columba\'s, Oxford (founded as a chaplaincy by the PCofE and the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Scotland" title="Church of Scotland">Church of Scotland</a> in 1908 and as a congregation of the PCofE in 1929), continue as congregations of the URC and university chaplaincies of the <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Scotland" title="Church of Scotland">Church of Scotland</a>.\n</p><p>In recent years a number of smaller denominations adopting Presbyterian forms of church government have organised in England, including the <a href="/wiki/International_Presbyterian_Church" title="International Presbyterian Church">International Presbyterian Church</a> planted by evangelical theologian <a href="/wiki/Francis_Schaeffer" title="Francis Schaeffer">Francis Schaeffer</a> of <a href="/wiki/L%27Abri" title="L&#39;Abri">L\'Abri Fellowship</a> in the 1970s, and the <a href="/wiki/Evangelical_Presbyterian_Church_in_England_and_Wales" title="Evangelical Presbyterian Church in England and Wales">Evangelical Presbyterian Church in England and Wales</a> founded in the North of England in the late 1980s.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Wales">Wales</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Presbyterianism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Wales">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>, Presbyterianism is represented by the <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_Church_of_Wales" title="Presbyterian Church of Wales">Presbyterian Church of Wales</a>, which was originally composed largely of Calvinistic Methodists who accepted Calvinist theology rather than the Arminianism of the Wesleyan Methodists. They broke off from the Church of England in 1811, ordaining their own ministers. They were originally known as the Calvinist Methodist connexion and in the 1920s it became alternatively known as the Presbyterian Church of Wales.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ireland">Ireland</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Presbyterianism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Ireland">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>Presbyterianism is the largest Protestant denomination in <a href="/wiki/Northern_Ireland" title="Northern Ireland">Northern Ireland</a> and the second largest on the island of Ireland (after the <a href="/wiki/Anglican" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglican">Anglican</a> <a href="/wiki/Church_of_Ireland" title="Church of Ireland">Church of Ireland</a>),<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> and was brought by Scottish <a href="/wiki/Plantation_of_Ulster" title="Plantation of Ulster">plantation settlers</a> to <a href="/wiki/Ulster" title="Ulster">Ulster</a> who had been strongly encouraged to emigrate by James VI of Scotland, later <a href="/wiki/James_I_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="James I of England">James I of Ireland and England</a>. An estimated 100,000 Scottish Presbyterians moved to the northern counties of Ireland between 1607 and the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Boyne" title="Battle of the Boyne">Battle of the Boyne</a> in 1690.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2007)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The Presbytery of Ulster was formed in 1642 separately from the established Anglican Church.  Presbyterians, along with <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic">Roman Catholics</a> in Ulster and the rest of Ireland, suffered under the discriminatory <a href="/wiki/Penal_Laws" class="mw-redirect" title="Penal Laws">Penal Laws</a> until they were revoked in the early 19th century.  Presbyterianism is represented in Ireland by the <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_Church_in_Ireland" title="Presbyterian Church in Ireland">Presbyterian Church in Ireland</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Free_Presbyterian_Church_of_Ulster" title="Free Presbyterian Church of Ulster">Free Presbyterian Church of Ulster</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Non-subscribing_Presbyterian_Church_of_Ireland" title="Non-subscribing Presbyterian Church of Ireland">Non-subscribing Presbyterian Church of Ireland</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Reformed_Presbyterian_Church_of_Ireland" title="Reformed Presbyterian Church of Ireland">Reformed Presbyterian Church of Ireland</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Evangelical_Presbyterian_Church_(Ireland)" title="Evangelical Presbyterian Church (Ireland)">Evangelical Presbyterian Church</a>.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="France">France</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Presbyterianism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: France">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<p>There is a Church of Scotland (Presbyterian) in central Paris: <a href="/wiki/The_Scots_Kirk,_Paris" title="The Scots Kirk, Paris">The Scots Kirk</a>, which is English-speaking, and is attended by many nationalities.  It maintains close links with the Church of Scotland in Scotland itself, as well as with the <a href="/wiki/Reformed_Church_of_France" title="Reformed Church of France">Reformed Church of France</a>.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Italy">Italy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Presbyterianism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Italy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Waldensians" title="Waldensians">Waldensians</a></div>\n<p>The Waldensian Evangelical Church (Chiesa Evangelica Valdese, CEV) is an Italian Protestant denomination.\nThe church was founded in the 12th century, and centuries later, after the Protestant Reformation, it adhered to Calvinist theology and became the Italian branch of the Presbyterian churches. As such, the church is a member of the World Communion of Reformed Churches.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="North_America">North America</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Presbyterianism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: North America">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Presbyterian_churches_in_North_America" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Presbyterian churches in North America">List of Presbyterian churches in North America</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Presbyterian_Family_Connections.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Presbyterian_Family_Connections.jpg/200px-Presbyterian_Family_Connections.jpg" width="200" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Presbyterian_Family_Connections.jpg/300px-Presbyterian_Family_Connections.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Presbyterian_Family_Connections.jpg/400px-Presbyterian_Family_Connections.jpg 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="648" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Presbyterian_Family_Connections.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Evolution of Presbyterianism in the United States</div></div></div>\n<p>Even before Presbyterianism spread with immigrants abroad from Scotland, there were divisions in the larger Presbyterian family.  Some later rejoined only to separate again.  In what some interpret as rueful self-reproach, some Presbyterians refer to the divided Presbyterian churches as the "Split P\'s".\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="United_States">United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Presbyterianism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/American_Presbyterianism" class="mw-redirect" title="American Presbyterianism">American Presbyterianism</a></div>\n<p>Presbyterianism first officially arrived in Colonial America in 1644 with the establishment of Christ\'s First Presbyterian Church in Hempstead, NY.  The Church was organized by the Rev. Richard Denton.\n</p><p>Another notable church was established in 1703 the first Presbytery in Philadelphia. In time, the presbytery would be joined by two more to form a synod (1717) and would eventually evolve into the <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_Church_in_the_United_States_of_America" title="Presbyterian Church in the United States of America">Presbyterian Church in the United States of America</a> in 1789. The nation\'s largest Presbyterian denomination, the <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_Church_(U.S.A.)" class="mw-redirect" title="Presbyterian Church (U.S.A.)">Presbyterian Church (U.S.A.)</a> \xe2\x80\x93 PC (USA) \xe2\x80\x93 can trace their heritage back to the original PCUSA, as can the <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_Church_in_America" title="Presbyterian Church in America">Presbyterian Church in America</a> (PCA), the <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Presbyterian_Church" title="Orthodox Presbyterian Church">Orthodox Presbyterian Church</a> (OPC), the <a href="/wiki/Bible_Presbyterian_Church" title="Bible Presbyterian Church">Bible Presbyterian Church</a> (BPC), the <a href="/wiki/Cumberland_Presbyterian_Church" title="Cumberland Presbyterian Church">Cumberland Presbyterian Church</a> (CPC), the <a href="/wiki/Cumberland_Presbyterian_Church_in_America" title="Cumberland Presbyterian Church in America">Cumberland Presbyterian Church in America</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Evangelical_Presbyterian_Church_(United_States)" title="Evangelical Presbyterian Church (United States)">Evangelical Presbyterian Church</a> (EPC), and the <a href="/wiki/Evangelical_Covenant_Order_of_Presbyterians" class="mw-redirect" title="Evangelical Covenant Order of Presbyterians">Evangelical Covenant Order of Presbyterians</a> (ECO).\n</p><p>Other Presbyterian bodies in the United States include the <a href="/wiki/Reformed_Presbyterian_Church_of_North_America" title="Reformed Presbyterian Church of North America">Reformed Presbyterian Church of North America</a> (RPCNA), the <a href="/wiki/Associate_Reformed_Presbyterian_Church" title="Associate Reformed Presbyterian Church">Associate Reformed Presbyterian Church</a> (ARP), the <a href="/wiki/Reformed_Presbyterian_Church_in_the_United_States" title="Reformed Presbyterian Church in the United States">Reformed Presbyterian Church in the United States</a> (RPCUS), the <a href="/wiki/Reformed_Presbyterian_Church_General_Assembly" title="Reformed Presbyterian Church General Assembly">Reformed Presbyterian Church General Assembly</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Reformed_Presbyterian_Church_%E2%80%93_Hanover_Presbytery" title="Reformed Presbyterian Church \xe2\x80\x93 Hanover Presbytery">Reformed Presbyterian Church \xe2\x80\x93 Hanover Presbytery</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Covenant_Presbyterian_Church" title="Covenant Presbyterian Church">Covenant Presbyterian Church</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Presbyterian_Reformed_Church_(North_America)" title="Presbyterian Reformed Church (North America)">Presbyterian Reformed Church</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Westminster_Presbyterian_Church_in_the_United_States" title="Westminster Presbyterian Church in the United States">Westminster Presbyterian Church in the United States</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Korean_American_Presbyterian_Church" title="Korean American Presbyterian Church">Korean American Presbyterian Church</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Free_Presbyterian_Church_of_North_America" title="Free Presbyterian Church of North America">Free Presbyterian Church of North America</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:The_First_Presbyterian_Church_Phoenix.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/The_First_Presbyterian_Church_Phoenix.JPG/220px-The_First_Presbyterian_Church_Phoenix.JPG" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/The_First_Presbyterian_Church_Phoenix.JPG/330px-The_First_Presbyterian_Church_Phoenix.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/The_First_Presbyterian_Church_Phoenix.JPG/440px-The_First_Presbyterian_Church_Phoenix.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="2048" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:The_First_Presbyterian_Church_Phoenix.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/First_Presbyterian_Church_(Phoenix,_Arizona)" title="First Presbyterian Church (Phoenix, Arizona)">First Presbyterian Church</a> in <a href="/wiki/Phoenix,_Arizona" title="Phoenix, Arizona">Phoenix, Arizona</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The territory within about a 50-mile (80&#160;km) radius of <a href="/wiki/Charlotte,_North_Carolina" title="Charlotte, North Carolina">Charlotte, North Carolina</a>, is historically the greatest concentration of Presbyterianism in the Southern United States, while an almost identical geographic area around <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh" title="Pittsburgh">Pittsburgh</a>, Pennsylvania, contains probably the largest number of Presbyterians in the entire nation.\n</p><p>The PC (USA), beginning with its predecessor bodies, has, in common with other so-called "mainline" Protestant denominations, experienced a significant decline in members in recent years.  Some estimates have placed that loss at nearly half in the last forty years.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Presbyterian influence, especially through <a href="/wiki/Princeton_theology" class="mw-redirect" title="Princeton theology">Princeton theology</a> can be traced in modern <a href="/wiki/Evangelicalism" title="Evangelicalism">Evangelicalism</a>. Balmer says that:\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p> Evangelicalism itself, I believe, is a quintessentially North American phenomenon, deriving as it did from the confluence of <a href="/wiki/Pietism" title="Pietism">Pietism</a>, Presbyterianism, and the vestiges of <a href="/wiki/Puritanism" class="mw-redirect" title="Puritanism">Puritanism</a>.  Evangelicalism picked up the peculiar characteristics from each strain \xe2\x80\x93 warmhearted spirituality from the Pietists (for instance), doctrinal precisionism from the Presbyterians, and individualistic introspection from the Puritans \xe2\x80\x93 even as the North American context itself has profoundly shaped the various manifestations of evangelicalism: fundamentalism, neo-evangelicalism, the holiness movement, Pentecostalism, the charismatic movement, and various forms of African-American and Hispanic evangelicalism.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>In the late 1800s, Presbyterian missionaries established a presence in what is now northern New Mexico.  This provided an alternative to the Catholicism, which was brought to the area by the Spanish Conquistadors and had remained unchanged.  The area experienced a "mini" reformation, in that many converts were made to Presbyterianism, prompting persecution. In some cases, the converts left towns and villages to establish their own neighboring villages.  The arrival of the United States to the area prompted the Catholic church to modernize and make efforts at winning the converts back, many of which did return.
